<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;padding-top:0;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Wittgenstein" title="Template:Wittgenstein"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Wittgenstein" title="Template talk:Wittgenstein"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Ludwig Josef Johann Wittgenstein</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ən/ 'on' in 'button'">ən</span><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/aɪ/ long 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <small>German:</small> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_German" title="Help:IPA for German">[ˈvɪtgənˌʃtaɪn]</a></span>; 26 April 1889 – 29 April 1951) was an Austrian-British philosopher who worked primarily in <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mathematics" title="Philosophy of mathematics">philosophy of mathematics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">philosophy of language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Time-8">[8]</a></sup> From 1929 to 1947, Wittgenstein taught at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-9">[9]</a></sup> During his lifetime he published just one slim book, the 75-page <i><a href="/wiki/Tractatus_Logico-Philosophicus" title="Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus">Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus</a></i> (1921), one article, one book review and a children's dictionary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="#Legacy">His voluminous manuscripts</a> were edited and published posthumously. <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Investigations" title="Philosophical Investigations">Philosophical Investigations</a></i> appeared as a book in 1953, and has since come to be recognised as one of the most important works of philosophy in the twentieth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> His teacher <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> described Wittgenstein as "the most perfect example I have ever known of genius as traditionally conceived; passionate, profound, intense, and dominating."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> into one of Europe's richest families, he inherited a large fortune from his father in 1913. He gave some considerable sums to poor artists. In a period of severe personal depression after the First World War, he then gave away his entire fortune to his brothers and sisters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Three of his brothers committed <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a>, with Wittgenstein contemplating it too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> He left academia several times—serving as an officer on the front line during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, where he was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_awards_and_decorations" title="Military awards and decorations">decorated</a> a number of times for his courage; teaching in schools in remote Austrian villages where he encountered controversy for hitting children when they made mistakes in mathematics; and working as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hospital_porter" title="Hospital porter">hospital porter</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in London where he told patients not to take the drugs they were prescribed while largely managing to keep secret the fact that he was one of the world's most famous philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> He described philosophy as "the only work that gives me real satisfaction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>His philosophy is often divided into an early period, exemplified by the <i>Tractatus</i>, and a later period, articulated in the <i>Philosophical Investigations</i>. The early Wittgenstein was concerned with the logical relationship between propositions and the world and believed that by providing an account of the logic underlying this relationship, he had solved all philosophical problems. The later Wittgenstein rejected many of the assumptions of the <i>Tractatus</i>, arguing that the meaning of words is best understood as their use within a given <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Language-game" title="Language-game">language-game</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Wittgensteins">The Wittgensteins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The Wittgensteins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Karl_Wittgenstein" title="Karl Wittgenstein">Karl Wittgenstein</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karl_Wittgenstein.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="277" data-file-width="177" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Karl_Wittgenstein.jpg/170px-Karl_Wittgenstein.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/Karl_Wittgenstein.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karl_Wittgenstein.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Karl_Wittgenstein" title="Karl Wittgenstein">Karl Wittgenstein</a> was one of the richest men in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bramann_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bramann-19">[19]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to a family tree prepared in Jerusalem after World War II, Wittgenstein's paternal great-grandfather was Moses Meier, a <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Jewish</a> land agent who lived with his wife, Brendel Simon, in <a href="/wiki/Bad_Laasphe" title="Bad Laasphe">Bad Laasphe</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Siegen-Wittgenstein" title="Siegen-Wittgenstein">Principality of Wittgenstein</a>, Westphalia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In July 1808, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> issued a decree that everyone, including Jews, must adopt an inheritable <a href="/wiki/Jewish_surname" title="Jewish surname">family surname</a>, and so Meier's son, also Moses, took the name of his employers, the <a href="/wiki/Sayn-Wittgenstein" title="Sayn-Wittgenstein">Sayn-Wittgensteins</a>, and became Moses Meier Wittgenstein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> His son, Hermann Christian Wittgenstein—who took the middle name "Christian" to distance himself from his Jewish background—married Fanny Figdor, also Jewish, who converted to <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> just before they married, and the couple founded a successful business trading in wool in Leipzig.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Ludwig's grandmother Fanny was a first cousin of the famous violinist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Joachim" title="Joseph Joachim">Joseph Joachim</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>They had 11 children—among them Wittgenstein's father. Karl Otto Clemens Wittgenstein (1847–1913) became an industrial tycoon, and by the late 1880s was one of the richest men in Europe, with an effective monopoly on Austria's steel cartel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bramann_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bramann-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated63_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated63-24">[24]</a></sup> Thanks to Karl, the Wittgensteins became the second wealthiest family in <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>, only behind the <a href="/wiki/Rothschild_family" title="Rothschild family">Rothschilds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated63_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated63-24">[24]</a></sup> As a result of his decision in 1898 to invest substantially in the Netherlands and in Switzerland as well as overseas, particularly in the US, the family was to an extent shielded from the hyperinflation that hit Austria in 1922.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> However, their wealth diminished due to post-1918 hyperinflation and subsequently during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, although even as late as 1938 they owned 13 mansions in Vienna alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wittgenstein" title="Paul Wittgenstein">Paul Wittgenstein</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gustav_Klimt_055.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2531" data-file-width="1256" height="343" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Gustav_Klimt_055.jpg/170px-Gustav_Klimt_055.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Gustav_Klimt_055.jpg/255px-Gustav_Klimt_055.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Gustav_Klimt_055.jpg/340px-Gustav_Klimt_055.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gustav_Klimt_055.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ludwig's sister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Stonborough-Wittgenstein" title="Margaret Stonborough-Wittgenstein">Margaret</a>, painted by <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Klimt" title="Gustav Klimt">Gustav Klimt</a> for her wedding portrait in 1905</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wittgenstein's mother was Leopoldine Maria Josefa Kalmus, known among friends as Poldi. Her father was a <a href="/wiki/Czechs" title="Czechs">Bohemian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jew" title="Jew">Jew</a> and her mother was <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austrian</a>-<a href="/wiki/Slovenes" title="Slovenes">Slovene</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>—she was Wittgenstein's only non-Jewish grandparent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> She was an aunt of the Nobel Prize laureate <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a> on her maternal side. Wittgenstein was born at 8:30 pm on 26 April 1889 in the so-called "Wittgenstein Palace" at Alleegasse 16, now the Argentinierstrasse, near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Charles%27s_Church,_Vienna" title="St. Charles's Church, Vienna">Karlskirche</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Karl and Poldi had nine children in all. There were four girls: Hermine, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Stonborough-Wittgenstein" title="Margaret Stonborough-Wittgenstein">Margaret</a> (Gretl), Helene, and a fourth daughter Dora who died as a baby; and five boys: Johannes (Hans), Kurt, Rudolf (Rudi), <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wittgenstein" title="Paul Wittgenstein">Paul</a>—who became a concert pianist despite losing an arm in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>—and Ludwig, who was the youngest of the family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartley16_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartley16-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The children were baptized as Catholics, received formal Catholic instruction, and raised in an exceptionally intense environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MW_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-MW-33">[33]</a></sup> The family was at the center of Vienna's cultural life; <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Walter" title="Bruno Walter">Bruno Walter</a> described the life at the Wittgensteins' palace as an "all-pervading atmosphere of humanity and culture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk8_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk8-34">[34]</a></sup> Karl was a leading patron of the arts, commissioning works by <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Rodin" title="Auguste Rodin">Auguste Rodin</a> and financing the city's exhibition hall and art gallery, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secession_hall_(Austria)" title="Secession hall (Austria)">Secession Building</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Klimt" title="Gustav Klimt">Gustav Klimt</a> painted Wittgenstein's sister for her wedding portrait, and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Johannes Brahms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Mahler" title="Gustav Mahler">Gustav Mahler</a> gave regular concerts in the family's numerous music rooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk8_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk8-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>For Wittgenstein, who highly valued precision and discipline, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contemporary_music" title="Contemporary music">contemporary music</a> was never considered acceptable at all. "Music," he said to his friend Drury in 1930, "came to a full stop with Brahms; and even in Brahms I can begin to hear the noise of machinery."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Wittgenstein himself had <a href="/wiki/Absolute_pitch" title="Absolute pitch">absolute pitch</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and his devotion to music remained vitally important to him throughout his life: he made frequent use of musical examples and metaphors in his philosophical writings, and was unusually adept at whistling lengthy and detailed musical passages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk443_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk443-38">[38]</a></sup> He also learnt to play the <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinet</a> in his thirties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk15_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk15-39">[39]</a></sup> A fragment of music (three bars), composed by Wittgenstein, was discovered in one of his 1931 notebooks, by Michael Nedo, Director of the Wittgenstein Institute in Cambridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_temperament_and_the_brothers.27_suicides">Family temperament and the brothers' suicides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Family temperament and the brothers' suicides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="423" data-file-width="296" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/37/The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg/170px-The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/37/The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg/255px-The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/37/The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
From left, Helene, Rudi, Hermine, Ludwig (the baby), <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Stonborough-Wittgenstein" title="Margaret Stonborough-Wittgenstein">Gretl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wittgenstein" title="Paul Wittgenstein">Paul</a>, Hans, and Kurt, around 1890</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ray_Monk" title="Ray Monk">Ray Monk</a> writes that Karl's aim was to turn his sons into captains of industry; they were not sent to school lest they acquire bad habits, but were educated at home to prepare them for work in Karl's industrial empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk11_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk11-41">[41]</a></sup> Three of the five brothers would later commit suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenny_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenny-42">[42]</a></sup> Psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Fitzgerald_(psychiatrist)" title="Michael Fitzgerald (psychiatrist)">Michael Fitzgerald</a> argues that Karl was a harsh perfectionist who lacked empathy, and that Wittgenstein's mother was anxious and insecure, unable to stand up to her husband.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald-43">[43]</a></sup> Johannes Brahms said of the family, whom he visited regularly: "They seemed to act towards one another as if they were at court."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated63_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated63-24">[24]</a></sup> The family appeared to have a strong streak of depression running through it. <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Gottlieb" title="Anthony Gottlieb">Anthony Gottlieb</a> tells a story about Paul practicing on one of the pianos in the Wittgensteins' main family mansion, when he suddenly shouted at Ludwig in the next room: "I cannot play when you are in the house, as I feel your scepticism seeping towards me from under the door!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gottlieb_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gottlieb-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tony_Attwood" title="Tony Attwood">Tony Attwood</a>, an international expert on <a href="/wiki/Asperger_syndrome" title="Asperger syndrome">Asperger's Syndrome</a>, has suggested that is is "highly likely" Wittgenstein lived with the condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein_siblings.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="195" data-file-width="295" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_siblings.jpg/220px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein_siblings.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9f/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_siblings.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein_siblings.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ludwig (bottom-right), Paul, and their sisters, late 1890s</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The family Palais housed seven grand pianos<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and each of the siblings pursued music "with an enthusiasm that, at times, bordered on the pathological."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The eldest brother, Hans, was hailed as a musical prodigy. At the age of four, writes <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Waugh" title="Alexander Waugh">Alexander Waugh</a>, Hans could identify the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler effect</a> in a passing siren as a quarter-tone drop in pitch, and at five started crying "Wrong! Wrong!" when two brass bands in a carnival played the same tune in different <a href="/wiki/Key_signature" title="Key signature">keys</a>. But he died in mysterious circumstances in May 1902, when he ran away to America and disappeared from a boat in <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_Bay" title="Chesapeake Bay">Chesapeake Bay</a>, most likely having committed suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two years later, aged 22 and studying chemistry at the <a href="/wiki/Prussian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Prussian Academy of Sciences">Berlin Academy</a>, the third eldest brother, Rudi, committed suicide in a Berlin bar. He had asked the pianist to play Thomas Koschat's "Verlassen, verlassen, verlassen bin ich" ("Forsaken, forsaken, forsaken am I"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> before mixing himself a drink of milk and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_cyanide" title="Potassium cyanide">potassium cyanide</a>. He had left several suicide notes, one to his parents that said he was grieving over the death of a friend, and another that referred to his "perverted disposition". It was reported at the time that he had sought advice from the <a href="/wiki/Scientific-Humanitarian_Committee" title="Scientific-Humanitarian Committee">Scientific-Humanitarian Committee</a>, an organization that was campaigning against <a href="/wiki/Paragraph_175" title="Paragraph 175">Paragraph 175</a> of the German Criminal Code, which prohibited homosexual sex. His father forbade the family from ever mentioning his name again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;"><i>I won't say 'See you tomorrow' because that would be like predicting the future, and I'm pretty sure I can't do that.</i></td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>— Wittgenstein, 1949<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The second eldest brother, Kurt, an officer and company director, shot himself on 27 October 1918 at the end of World War I, when the Austrian troops he was commanding refused to obey his orders and deserted <i>en masse</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk11_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk11-41">[41]</a></sup> According to Gottlieb, Hermine had said Kurt seemed to carry "...the germ of disgust for life within himself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Later Wittgenstein wrote: "I ought to have... become a star in the sky. Instead of which I have remained stuck on earth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1903.E2.80.931906:_Realschule_in_Linz">1903–1906: Realschule in Linz</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1903–1906: Realschule in Linz">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Realschule_in_Linz">Realschule in Linz</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Realschule in Linz">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Realschule_in_Linz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="221" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/44/Realschule_in_Linz.jpg/170px-Realschule_in_Linz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/44/Realschule_in_Linz.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Realschule_in_Linz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Realschule" title="Realschule">Realschule</a></i> in Linz</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wittgenstein was taught by private tutors at home until he was fourteen years old. Subsequently, for three years, he attended a school. After the deaths of Hans and Rudi, Karl relented, and allowed Paul and Ludwig to be sent to school. Waugh writes that it was too late for Wittgenstein to pass his exams for the more academic <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">Gymnasium</a> in Wiener Neustadt; having had no formal schooling, he failed his entrance exam and only barely managed after extra tutoring to pass the exam for the more technically oriented <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K.u.k." title="K.u.k.">k.u.k.</a> <a href="/wiki/Realschule" title="Realschule">Realschule</a> in <a href="/wiki/Linz" title="Linz">Linz</a>, a small state school with 300 pupils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waugh33_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waugh33-54">[54]</a></sup> In 1903, when he was 14, he began his three years of formal schooling there, lodging nearby in term time with the family of Dr. Josef Strigl, a teacher at the local gymnasium, the family giving him the nickname Luki.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGuinness51_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGuinness51-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandgruber_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sandgruber-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>On starting at the Realschule, Wittgenstein had been moved forward a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Brigitte_Hamann" title="Brigitte Hamann">Brigitte Hamann</a> writes that he stood out from the other boys: he spoke an unusually pure form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_German" title="High German">High German</a> with a stutter, dressed elegantly, and was sensitive and unsociable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamann15_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamann15-58">[58]</a></sup> Monk writes that the other boys made fun of him, singing after him: "<i>Wittgenstein wandelt wehmütig widriger Winde wegen Wienwärts</i>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk15_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk15-39">[39]</a></sup> ("Wittgenstein strolls wistfully Vienna-wards due to adverse winds"). In his leaving certificate, he received a top mark (5) in religious studies; a 2 for conduct and English, 3 for French, geography, history, mathematics and physics, and 4 for German, chemistry, geometry and freehand drawing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGuinness51_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-McGuinness51-55">[55]</a></sup> He had particular difficulty with spelling and failed his written German exam because of it. He wrote in 1931: "My bad spelling in youth, up to the age of about 18 or 19, is connected with the whole of the rest of my character (my weakness in study)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGuinness51_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-McGuinness51-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Faith">Faith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Faith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wittgenstein was baptized as an infant by a Catholic priest and received formal instruction in Catholic doctrine as a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MW_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-MW-33">[33]</a></sup> It was while he was at the <i>Realschule</i> that he decided he had lost his faith in God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk18_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk18-59">[59]</a></sup> He nevertheless believed in the importance of the idea of confession. He wrote in his diaries about having made a major confession to his oldest sister, Hermine, while he was at the <i>Realschule</i>; Monk writes that it may have been about his loss of faith. He also discussed it with Gretl, his other sister, who directed him to <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_as_Will_and_Representation" title="The World as Will and Representation">The World as Will and Representation</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk18_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk18-59">[59]</a></sup> As a teenager, Wittgenstein adopted Schopenhauer's epistemological idealism. However, after his study of the philosophy of mathematics, he abandoned epistemological idealism for <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a>'s conceptual realism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-60">[60]</a></sup> In later years, Wittgenstein was highly dismissive of Schopenhauer, describing him as an ultimately "shallow" thinker: "Schopenhauer has quite a crude mind ... where real depth starts, his comes to an end."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wittgenstein's faith would undergo developmental transformations over time, much like his philosophical ideas; his relationship with Christianity and religion, in general, for which he professed a sincere and devoted reverence, would eventually flourish. Undoubtedly, amongst other Christian thinkers, Wittgenstein was influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Augustine" title="St. Augustine">St. Augustine</a>, with whom he would occasionally converse in his <i>Philosophical Investigations.</i> Philosophically, Wittgenstein's thought shows fundamental alignment with religious discourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> For example, Wittgenstein would become one of the century's fiercest critics of <a href="/wiki/Scientism" title="Scientism">Scientism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>With age, his deepening Christianity led to many religious elucidations and clarifications, as he untangled language problems in religion, attacking, for example, the temptation to think of God's existence as a matter of scientific evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In 1947, finding it more difficult to work, he wrote, "I have had a letter from an old friend in Austria, a priest. In it he says that he hopes my work will go well, if it should be God's will. Now that is all I want: if it should be God's will."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-65">[65]</a></sup> In Wittgenstein's <i>Culture and Value</i>, it is found, "Is what I am doing [my work in philosophy] really worth the effort? Yes, but only if a light shines on it from above." His close friend <a href="/wiki/Norman_Malcolm" title="Norman Malcolm">Norman Malcolm</a> would write, "Wittgenstein’s mature life was strongly marked by religious thought and feeling. I am inclined to think that he was more deeply religious than are many people who correctly regard themselves as religious believers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> At last, Wittgenstein writes, "Bach wrote on the title page of his <i>Orgelbuechlein</i>, ‘To the glory of the most high God, and that my neighbour may be benefited thereby.’ That is what I would have liked to say about my work."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_of_Otto_Weininger">Influence of Otto Weininger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Influence of Otto Weininger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="513" data-file-width="437" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg/170px-OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg/255px-OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg/340px-OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Austrian philosopher <a href="/wiki/Otto_Weininger" title="Otto Weininger">Otto Weininger</a> (1880–1903)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While a student at the <i>Realschule</i>, Wittgenstein was influenced by Austrian philosopher <a href="/wiki/Otto_Weininger" title="Otto Weininger">Otto Weininger</a>'s 1903 book <i>Geschlecht und Charakter</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Sex_and_Character" title="Sex and Character">Sex and Character</a></i>). Weininger (1880–1903), who was also Jewish, argued that the concepts male and female exist only as <a href="/wiki/Platonic_realism" title="Platonic realism">Platonic forms</a>, and that Jews tend to embody the platonic femininity. Whereas men are basically rational, women operate only at the level of their emotions and sexual organs. Jews, Weininger argued, are similar, saturated with femininity, with no sense of right and wrong, and no soul. Weininger argues that man must choose between his masculine and feminine sides, consciousness and unconsciousness, Platonic love and sexuality. Love and sexual desire stand in contradiction, and love between a woman and a man is therefore doomed to misery or immorality. The only life worth living is the spiritual one—to live as a woman or a Jew means one has no right to live at all; the choice is genius or death. Weininger committed suicide, shooting himself in 1903, shortly after publishing the book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk19_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk19-67">[67]</a></sup> Many years later, as a professor at Cambridge, Wittgenstein distributed copies of Weininger's book to his bemused academic colleagues. He said that Weininger's arguments were wrong, but that it was the way they were wrong that was interesting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_background_and_Hitler">Jewish background and Hitler</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Jewish background and Hitler">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Austria" title="History of the Jews in Austria">History of the Jews in Austria</a></div>
<p>There is much debate about the extent to which Wittgenstein and his siblings, who were of 3/4 Jewish descent, saw themselves as Jews. The issue has arisen in particular regarding Wittgenstein's schooldays, because <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> was at the same school for part of the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGuinnessStern_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGuinnessStern-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Goldstein" title="Laurence Goldstein">Laurence Goldstein</a> argues it is "overwhelmingly probable" the boys met each other: that Hitler would have disliked Wittgenstein, a "stammering, precocious, precious, aristocratic upstart ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Other commentators have dismissed as irresponsible and uninformed any suggestion that Wittgenstein's wealth and unusual personality may have fed Hitler's antisemitism, in part because there is no indication that Hitler would have seen Wittgenstein as Jewish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wittgenstein and Hitler were born just six days apart, though Hitler had been held back a year, while Wittgenstein was moved forward by one, so they ended up two grades apart at the <i>Realschule</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Monk estimates they were both at the school during the 1904–1905 school year, but says there is no evidence they had anything to do with each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Several commentators have argued that a school photograph of Hitler may show Wittgenstein in the lower left corner,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-image_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-image-74">[74]</a></sup> but Hamann says the photograph stems from 1900 or 1901, before Wittgenstein's time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his own writings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Wittgenstein frequently referred to himself as Jewish, at times as part of an apparent self-flagellation. For example, while berating himself for being a "reproductive" as opposed to "productive" thinker, he attributed this to his own Jewish sense of identity, writing: "The saint is the only Jewish genius. Even the greatest Jewish thinker is no more than talented. (Myself for instance)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> While Wittgenstein would later claim that "[m]y thoughts are 100% Hebraic,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> as <a href="/wiki/Hans_Sluga" title="Hans Sluga">Hans Sluga</a> has argued, if so, "His was a self-doubting Judaism, which had always the possibility of collapsing into a destructive self-hatred (as it did in <a href="/wiki/Otto_Weininger" title="Otto Weininger">Weininger's</a> case) but which also held an immense promise of innovation and genius."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1906.E2.80.931913:_University">1906–1913: University</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1906–1913: University">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering_at_Berlin_and_Manchester">Engineering at Berlin and Manchester</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Engineering at Berlin and Manchester">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1088" data-file-width="1720" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg/220px-1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg/330px-1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg/440px-1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berlin_Institute_of_Technology" title="Berlin Institute of Technology">Technische Hochschule</a> in <a href="/wiki/Charlottenburg" title="Charlottenburg">Charlottenburg</a>, Berlin</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>He began his studies in mechanical engineering at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berlin_Institute_of_Technology" title="Berlin Institute of Technology">Technische Hochschule</a> in <a href="/wiki/Charlottenburg" title="Charlottenburg">Charlottenburg</a>, Berlin, on 23 October 1906, lodging with the family of professor Dr. Jolles. He attended for three semesters, and was awarded a <a href="/wiki/Diploma" title="Diploma">diploma</a> (<i>Abgangzeugnis</i>) on 5 May 1908. During his time at the Institute, Wittgenstein developed an interest in aeronautics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> He arrived at the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_University_of_Manchester" title="Victoria University of Manchester">Victoria University of Manchester</a> in the spring of 1908 to do his doctorate, full of plans for aeronautical projects, including designing and flying his own plane. He conducted research into the behavior of kites in the upper atmosphere, experimenting at a meteorological observation site near Glossop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grouse_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grouse-81">[81]</a></sup> He also worked on the design of a propeller with small jet engines on the end of its blades, something he patented in 1911, and which earned him a research studentship from the university in the autumn of 1908.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk30_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk30-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Grouse_Inn_near_Glossop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="305" data-file-width="529" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/The_Grouse_Inn_near_Glossop.jpg/220px-The_Grouse_Inn_near_Glossop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/The_Grouse_Inn_near_Glossop.jpg/330px-The_Grouse_Inn_near_Glossop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/The_Grouse_Inn_near_Glossop.jpg/440px-The_Grouse_Inn_near_Glossop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Grouse_Inn_near_Glossop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wittgenstein stayed at the Grouse Inn in 1908 while engaged in research near <a href="/wiki/Glossop" title="Glossop">Glossop</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grouse_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grouse-81">[81]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It was at this time that he became interested in the <a href="/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundations of mathematics</a>, particularly after reading <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Principles_of_Mathematics" title="The Principles of Mathematics">The Principles of Mathematics</a></i> (1903), and <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Foundations_of_Arithmetic" title="The Foundations of Arithmetic">The Foundations of Arithmetic</a></i>, vol. 1 (1893) and vol. 2 (1903).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Wittgenstein's sister Hermine said he became obsessed with mathematics as a result, and was anyway losing interest in aeronautics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk30_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk30-82">[82]</a></sup> He decided instead that he needed to study logic and the foundations of mathematics, describing himself as in a "constant, indescribable, almost pathological state of agitation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk30_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Monk30-82">[82]</a></sup> In the summer of 1911 he visited Frege at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Jena" title="University of Jena">University of Jena</a> to show him some philosophy of mathematics and logic he had written, and to ask whether it was worth pursuing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk36_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk36-84">[84]</a></sup> He wrote: "I was shown into Frege's study. Frege was a small, neat man with a pointed beard who bounced around the room as he talked. He absolutely wiped the floor with me, and I felt very depressed; but at the end he said 'You must come again', so I cheered up. I had several discussions with him after that. Frege would never talk about anything but logic and mathematics, if I started on some other subject, he would say something polite and then plunge back into logic and mathematics."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kanterian36_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kanterian36-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrival_at_Cambridge">Arrival at Cambridge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Arrival at Cambridge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WrenLibraryInterior.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="809" data-file-width="1247" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/WrenLibraryInterior.jpg/200px-WrenLibraryInterior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/WrenLibraryInterior.jpg/300px-WrenLibraryInterior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/WrenLibraryInterior.jpg/400px-WrenLibraryInterior.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WrenLibraryInterior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Wren_Library" title="Wren Library">Wren Library</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wittgenstein wanted to study with Frege, but Frege suggested he attend the University of Cambridge to study under Russell, so on 18 October 1911 Wittgenstein arrived unannounced at Russell's rooms in <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ConnorRobertson_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-ConnorRobertson-86">[86]</a></sup> Russell was having tea with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C._K._Ogden" title="C. K. Ogden">C. K. Ogden</a>, when, according to Russell, "an unknown German appeared, speaking very little English but refusing to speak German. He turned out to be a man who had learned engineering at Charlottenburg, but during this course had acquired, by himself, a passion for the philosophy of mathematics &amp; has now come to Cambridge on purpose to hear me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk36_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk36-84">[84]</a></sup> He was soon not only attending Russell's lectures, but dominating them. The lectures were poorly attended and Russell often found himself lecturing only to <a href="/wiki/C._D._Broad" title="C. D. Broad">C. D. Broad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Harold_Neville" title="Eric Harold Neville">E. H. Neville</a>, and H. T. J. Norton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk36_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Monk36-84">[84]</a></sup> Wittgenstein started following him after lectures back to his rooms to discuss more philosophy, until it was time for the evening meal in <a href="/wiki/Formal_(university)#Terminology" title="Formal (university)">Hall</a>. Russell grew irritated; he wrote to his lover <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lady_Ottoline_Morrell" title="Lady Ottoline Morrell">Lady Ottoline Morrell</a>: "My German friend threatens to be an infliction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGuinness88_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGuinness88-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Russell soon came to believe that Wittgenstein was a genius, especially after he had examined Wittgenstein's written work. He wrote in November 1911 that he had at first thought Wittgenstein might be a crank, but soon decided he was a genius: "Some of his early views made the decision difficult. He maintained, for example, at one time that all existential propositions are meaningless. This was in a lecture room, and I invited him to consider the proposition: 'There is no hippopotamus in this room at present.' When he refused to believe this, I looked under all the desks without finding one; but he remained unconvinced."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGuinness88_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-McGuinness88-87">[87]</a></sup> Three months after Wittgenstein's arrival Russell told Morrell: "I love him &amp; feel he will solve the problems I am too old to solve ... He is <i>the</i> young man one hopes for."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk41_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk41-88">[88]</a></sup> The role-reversal between him and Wittgenstein was such that he wrote in 1916, after Wittgenstein had criticized Russell's own work: "His criticism, 'tho I don't think he realized it at the time, was an event of first-rate importance in my life, and affected everything I have done since. I saw that he was right, and I saw that I could not hope ever again to do fundamental work in philosophy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cambridge_Moral_Sciences_Club_and_Apostles">Cambridge Moral Sciences Club and Apostles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cambridge Moral Sciences Club and Apostles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russell1907-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="414" data-file-width="316" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Russell1907-2.jpg/150px-Russell1907-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Russell1907-2.jpg/225px-Russell1907-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Russell1907-2.jpg/300px-Russell1907-2.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Russell1907-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>, in 1907</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1912 Wittgenstein joined the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Moral_Sciences_Club" title="Cambridge University Moral Sciences Club">Cambridge Moral Sciences Club</a>, an influential discussion group for philosophy dons and students, delivering his first paper there on 29 November that year, a four-minute talk defining philosophy as "all those primitive propositions which are assumed as true without proof by the various sciences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> He dominated the society and stopped attending entirely in the early 1930s after complaints that he gave no one else a chance to speak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edmonds22_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edmonds22-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>The club became infamous within popular philosophy because of a meeting on 25 October 1946 at <a href="/wiki/R._B._Braithwaite" title="R. B. Braithwaite">Richard Braithwaite</a>'s rooms in <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College,_Cambridge" title="King's College, Cambridge">King's</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a>, another Viennese philosopher, had been invited as the guest speaker. Popper's paper was <i>Are there philosophical problems?</i>, in which he struck up a position against Wittgenstein's, contending that problems in philosophy are real, not just linguistic puzzles as Wittgenstein argued. Accounts vary as to what happened next, but Wittgenstein apparently started waving a hot poker, demanding that Popper give him an example of a moral rule. Popper offered one—"Not to threaten visiting speakers with pokers"—at which point Russell told Wittgenstein he had misunderstood and Wittgenstein left. Popper maintained that Wittgenstein 'stormed out', but it had become accepted practice for him to leave early (because of his aforementioned ability to dominate discussion). It was the only time the philosophers, three of the most eminent in the world, were ever in the same room together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The minutes record that the meeting was "charged to an unusual degree with a spirit of controversy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a> also invited him to join the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Apostles" title="Cambridge Apostles">Cambridge Apostles</a>, an elite secret society formed in 1820, which both Russell and <a href="/wiki/G._E._Moore" title="G. E. Moore">G. E. Moore</a> had joined as students, but Wittgenstein did not enjoy it and attended infrequently. Russell had been worried that Wittgenstein would not appreciate the group's unseriousness, style of humour, or the fact that the members were in love with one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> He was admitted in 1912 but resigned almost immediately because he could not tolerate the level of the discussion on the Hearth Rug; they took him back though in the 1920s when he returned to Cambridge. (He also had trouble tolerating the discussions in the Moral Sciences Club.)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_orientation_and_relationship_with_David_Pinsent">Sexual orientation and relationship with David Pinsent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sexual orientation and relationship with David Pinsent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wittgenstein had romantic relations with both men and women. He is generally believed to have fallen in love with at least three men: <a href="/wiki/David_Pinsent" title="David Pinsent">David Hume Pinsent</a> in 1912, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Skinner" title="Francis Skinner">Francis Skinner</a> in 1930, and Ben Richards in the late 1940s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> He later revealed that, as a teenager in Vienna, he had had an affair with a woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Additionally, in the 1920s Wittgenstein became infatuated with a young Swiss woman, Marguerite Respinger, modelling a sculpture of her and proposing marriage, albeit on condition that they did not have children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wittgenstein's <a href="/wiki/Platonic_love" title="Platonic love">platonic relationship</a> with David Pinsent (1891–1918) occurred during an intellectually formative period, and is well documented. Bertrand Russell introduced Wittgenstein to Pinsent in the summer of 1912. A mathematics undergraduate and descendant of <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, Pinsent soon became Wittgenstein's closest friend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The men worked together on experiments in the psychology laboratory about the role of rhythm in the appreciation of music, and Wittgenstein delivered a paper on the subject to the <a href="/wiki/British_Psychological_Society" title="British Psychological Society">British Psychological Association</a> in Cambridge in 1912. They also travelled together, including to Iceland in September 1912—the expenses paid by Wittgenstein, including <a href="/wiki/First_class_travel" title="First class travel">first class travel</a>, the hiring of a private train, and new clothes and spending money for Pinsent—and later to Norway. Pinsent's diaries provide valuable insights into Wittgenstein's personality - sensitive, nervous and attuned to the tiniest slight or change in mood from Pinsent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> In his diaries Pinsent wrote about shopping for furniture with Wittgenstein in Cambridge when the latter was given rooms in Trinity; most of what they found in the stores was not minimalist enough for Wittgenstein's aesthetics: "I went and helped him interview a lot of furniture at various shops ... It was rather amusing: he is terribly fastidious and we led the shopman a frightful dance, Vittgenstein [sic] ejaculating "No—Beastly!" to 90 percent of what he shewed [archaic spelling] us!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kanterian40_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kanterian40-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>He wrote in May 1912 that Wittgenstein had just begun to study the history of philosophy: "He expresses the most naive surprise that all the philosophers he once worshipped in ignorance are after all stupid and dishonest and make disgusting mistakes!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kanterian40_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kanterian40-100">[100]</a></sup> The last time they saw each other was on 8 October 1913 at Lordswood House in Birmingham, then residence of the Pinsent family: "I got up at 6.15 to see Ludwig off. He had to go very early – back to Cambridge – as he has lots to do there. I saw him off from the house in a taxi at 7.0 – to catch a 7.30 am train from New St Station. It was sad parting from him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wright-101">[101]</a></sup> Wittgenstein left to live in Norway.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1913.E2.80.931920:_World_War_I_and_the_Tractatus">1913–1920: World War I and the <i>Tractatus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 1913–1920: World War I and the Tractatus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_on_Logik">Work on <i>Logik</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Work on Logik">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg/220px-Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg/330px-Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg/440px-Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Entries from October 1914 in Wittgenstein's diary, on display at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wren_Library,_Cambridge" title="Wren Library, Cambridge">Wren Library</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Karl Wittgenstein died on 20 January 1913, and after receiving his inheritance Wittgenstein became one of the wealthiest men in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> He donated some of his money, at first anonymously, to Austrian artists and writers, including <a href="/wiki/Rainer_Maria_Rilke" title="Rainer Maria Rilke">Rainer Maria Rilke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georg_Trakl" title="Georg Trakl">Georg Trakl</a>. Wittgenstein came to feel that he could not get to the heart of his most fundamental questions while surrounded by other academics, and so in 1913 he retreated to the village of <a href="/wiki/Skjolden" title="Skjolden">Skjolden</a> in Norway, where he rented the second floor of a house for the winter. He later saw this as one of the most productive periods of his life, writing <i>Logik</i> (<i>Notes on Logic</i>), the predecessor of much of the <i>Tractatus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ConnorRobertson_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-ConnorRobertson-86">[86]</a></sup> While in Norway, Wittgenstein learned <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a> to converse with the local villagers, and <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> to read the works of the Danish philosopher <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="Søren Kierkegaard">Søren Kierkegaard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>At Wittgenstein's insistence, Moore, who was now a Cambridge don, visited him in Norway in 1914, reluctantly because Wittgenstein exhausted him. <a href="/wiki/David_Edmonds_(philosopher)" title="David Edmonds (philosopher)">David Edmonds</a> and John Eidinow write that Wittgenstein regarded Moore, an internationally known philosopher, as an example of how far someone could get in life with "absolutely no intelligence whatever."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> In Norway it was clear that Moore was expected to act as Wittgenstein's secretary, taking down his notes, with Wittgenstein falling into a rage when Moore got something wrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edmonds45_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edmonds45-105">[105]</a></sup> When he returned to Cambridge, Moore asked the university to consider accepting <i>Logik</i> as sufficient for a bachelor's degree, but they refused, saying it wasn't formatted properly: no footnotes, no preface. Wittgenstein was furious, writing to Moore in May 1914: "If I am not worth your making an exception for me <i>even in some STUPID details</i> then I may as well go to Hell directly; and if I <i>am</i> worth it and you don't do it then—by God—<i>you</i> might go there."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Moore was apparently distraught; he wrote in his diary that he felt sick and could not get the letter out of his head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGuinness200_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGuinness200-107">[107]</a></sup> The two did not speak again until 1929.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edmonds45_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-Edmonds45-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_service">Military service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Military service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25,_Isonzo-Schlacht,_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="589" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25,_Isonzo-Schlacht,_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Austro-Hungarian supply line over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vr%C5%A1i%C4%8D" title="Vršič">Vršič</a> pass, on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Campaign_(World_War_I)" title="Italian Campaign (World War I)">Italian front</a>, October 1917</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the outbreak of World War I, Wittgenstein immediately volunteered for the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Army" title="Austro-Hungarian Army">Austro-Hungarian Army</a>, despite being eligible for a medical exemption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> He served first on a ship and then in an artillery workshop 'several miles from the action'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> He was wounded in an accidental explosion, and hospitalised to <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> In March 1916, he was posted to a fighting unit on the front line of the Russian front, as part of the Austrian 7th Army, where his unit was involved in some of the heaviest fighting, defending against the <a href="/wiki/Brusilov_Offensive" title="Brusilov Offensive">Brusilov Offensive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk137_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk137-112">[112]</a></sup> Wittgenstein directed the fire of his own artillery from an observation post in no-man's land against Allied troops - one of the most dangerous jobs there was, since he was targeted by enemy snipers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> In action against British troops, he was decorated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_Merit_Medal_(Austria%E2%80%93Hungary)" title="Military Merit Medal (Austria–Hungary)">Military Merit</a> with Swords on the Ribbon, and was commended by the army for "His exceptionally courageous behaviour, calmness, sang-froid, and heroism," that "won the total admiration of the troops."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> In January 1917, he was sent as a member of a <a href="/wiki/Howitzer" title="Howitzer">howitzer</a> regiment to the Russian front, where he won several more medals for bravery including the Silver Medal for Valour, First Class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk137_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk137-112">[112]</a></sup> In 1918, he was promoted to lieutenant and sent to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_Campaign_(World_War_I)" title="Italian Campaign (World War I)">Italian front</a> as part of an artillery regiment. For his part in the final Austrian offensive of June 1918, he was recommended for the Gold Medal for Valour, one of the highest honours in the Austrian army, but was instead awarded the Band of the Military Service Medal with Swords — it being decided that this particular action, although extraordinarily brave, had been insufficiently consequential to merit the highest honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout the war, he kept notebooks in which he frequently wrote philosophical reflections alongside personal remarks, including his contempt for the character of the other soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klagge2010_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klagge2010-116">[116]</a></sup> He discovered <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>'s 1896 <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Gospel_in_Brief&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Gospel in Brief (page does not exist)">The Gospel in Brief</a></i> at a bookshop in <a href="/wiki/Tarn%C3%B3w" title="Tarnów">Tarnów</a>, and carried it everywhere, recommending it to anyone in distress, to the point where he became known to his fellow soldiers as "the man with the gospels."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> In 1916 Wittgenstein read <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dostoevsky" title="Dostoevsky">Dostoevsky</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brothers_Karamazov" title="The Brothers Karamazov">The Brothers Karamazov</a></i> so often that he knew whole passages of it by heart, particularly the speeches of the elder Zosima, who represented for him a powerful Christian ideal, a holy man "who could see directly into the souls of other people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iain_King" title="Iain King">Iain King</a> has suggested his writing changed substantially in 1916, when he started confronting much greater dangers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Russell said he returned from the war a changed man, one with a deeply mystical and ascetic attitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Completion_of_the_Tractatus">Completion of the <i>Tractatus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Completion of the Tractatus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the summer of 1918 Wittgenstein took military leave and went to stay in one of his family's Vienna summer houses, Neuwaldegg. It was there in August 1918 that he completed the <i>Tractatus</i>, which he submitted with the title <i>Der Satz</i> (German: proposition, sentence, phrase, set, but also "leap") to the publishers Jahoda and Siegel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartley33_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartley33-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>A series of events around this time left him deeply upset. On 13 August, his uncle Paul died. On 25 October, he learned that Jahoda and Siegel had decided not to publish the <i>Tractatus</i>, and on 27 October, his brother Kurt killed himself, the third of his brothers to commit suicide. It was around this time he received a letter from David Pinsent's mother to say that Pinsent had been killed in a plane crash on 8 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Wittgenstein was distraught to the point of being suicidal. He was sent back to the Italian front after his leave and, as a result of the defeat of the Austrian army, was captured by <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied forces</a> on 3 November in <a href="/wiki/Trentino" title="Trentino">Trentino</a>. He subsequently spent nine months in an Italian prisoner of war camp.</p>
<p>He returned to his family in Vienna on 25 August 1919, by all accounts physically and mentally spent. He apparently talked incessantly about suicide, terrifying his sisters and brother Paul. He decided to do two things: to enroll in teacher training college as an elementary school teacher, and to get rid of his fortune. In 1914, it had been providing him with an income of 300,000 <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_krone" title="Austro-Hungarian krone">Kronen</a> a year, but by 1919 was worth a great deal more, with a sizable portfolio of investments in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. He divided it among his siblings, except for Margarete, insisting that it not be held in trust for him. His family saw him as ill, and acquiesced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartley33_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bartley33-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1920.E2.80.931928:_Teaching.2C_the_Tractatus.2C_Haus_Wittgenstein">1920–1928: Teaching, the <i>Tractatus</i>, Haus Wittgenstein</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 1920–1928: Teaching, the Tractatus, Haus Wittgenstein">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teacher_training_in_Vienna">Teacher training in Vienna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Teacher training in Vienna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In September 1919 he enrolled in the <i>Lehrerbildungsanstalt</i> (teacher training college) in the <i>Kundmanngasse</i> in Vienna. His sister Hermine said that Wittgenstein working as an elementary teacher was like using a precision instrument to open crates, but the family decided not to interfere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk169_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk169-123">[123]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bernhard" title="Thomas Bernhard">Thomas Bernhard</a>, more critically, wrote of this period in Wittgenstein's life: "the multi-millionaire as a village schoolmaster is surely a piece of perversity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teaching_posts_in_Austria">Teaching posts in Austria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Teaching posts in Austria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the summer of 1920, Wittgenstein worked as a gardener for a monastery. At first he applied, under a false name, for a teaching post at Reichenau, was awarded the job, but he declined it when his identity was discovered. As a teacher, he wished to no longer be recognized as a member of the famous Wittgenstein family. In response, his brother Paul wrote:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"It is out of the question, really completely out of the question, that anybody bearing our name and whose elegant and gentle upbringing can be seen a thousand paces off, would not be identified as a member of our family... That one can neither simulate nor dissimulate anything including a refined education I need hardly tell you."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1920, Wittgenstein was given his first job as a primary school teacher in <a href="/wiki/Trattenbach" title="Trattenbach">Trattenbach</a>, under his real name, in a remote village of a few hundred people. His first letters describe it as beautiful, but in October 1921, he wrote to Russell: "I am still at Trattenbach, surrounded, as ever, by odiousness and baseness. I know that human beings on the average are not worth much anywhere, but here they are much more good-for-nothing and irresponsible than elsewhere."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> He was soon the object of gossip among the villagers, who found him eccentric at best. He did not get on well with the other teachers; when he found his lodgings too noisy, he made a bed for himself in the school kitchen. He was an enthusiastic teacher, offering late-night extra tuition to several of the students, something that did not endear him to the parents, though some of them came to adore him; his sister Hermine occasionally watched him teach and said the students "literally crawled over each other in their desire to be chosen for answers or demonstrations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>To the less able, it seems that he became something of a tyrant. The first two hours of each day were devoted to mathematics, hours that Monk writes some of the pupils recalled years later with horror.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> They reported that he caned the boys and boxed their ears, and also that he pulled the girls' hair;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartley107_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartley107-129">[129]</a></sup> This was not unusual at the time for boys, but for the villagers he went too far in doing it to the girls too; girls were not expected to understand algebra, much less have their ears boxed over it. The violence apart, Monk writes that he quickly became a village legend, shouting "<i>Krautsalat!</i>" ("coleslaw" - i.e. shredded cabbage) when the headmaster played the piano, and "Nonsense!" when a priest was answering children's questions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Publication_of_the_Tractatus">Publication of the <i>Tractatus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Publication of the Tractatus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
