<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;"><i>The whole modern conception of the world is founded on the illusion that the so-called laws of nature are the explanations of natural phenomena.</i>
<p><i>Thus people today stop at the laws of nature, treating them as something inviolable, just as God and Fate were treated in past ages. And in fact both were right and both wrong; though the view of the ancients is clearer insofar as they have an acknowledged terminus, while the modern system tries to make it look as if everything were explained.</i></p>
</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>— Wittgenstein, <a href="/wiki/Tractatus_Logico-Philosophicus" title="Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus">Tractatus</a>, 6.371-2</cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>While Wittgenstein was living in isolation in rural Austria, the <i><a href="/wiki/Tractatus_Logico-Philosophicus" title="Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus">Tractatus</a></i> was published to considerable interest, first in German in 1921 as <i>Logisch-Philosophische Abhandlung</i>, part of <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Ostwald" title="Wilhelm Ostwald">Wilhelm Ostwald</a>'s journal <i>Annalen der Naturphilosophie</i>, though Wittgenstein was not happy with the result and called it a pirate edition. Russell had agreed to write an introduction to explain why it was important, because it was otherwise unlikely to have been published: it was difficult if not impossible to understand, and Wittgenstein was unknown in philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> In a letter to Russell, Wittgenstein wrote "The main point is the theory of what can be expressed (gesagt) by prop[osition]s—i.e. by language—(and, which comes to the same thing, what can be <i>thought</i>) and what can not be expressed by pro[position]s, but only shown (gezeigt); which, I believe, is the cardinal problem of philosophy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> But Wittgenstein was not happy with Russell's help. He had lost faith in Russell, finding him glib and his philosophy mechanistic, and felt he had fundamentally misunderstood the <i>Tractatus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>An English translation was prepared in Cambridge by <a href="/wiki/Frank_P._Ramsey" title="Frank P. Ramsey">Frank Ramsey</a>, a mathematics undergraduate at King's commissioned by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C._K._Ogden" title="C. K. Ogden">C. K. Ogden</a>. It was Moore who suggested <i>Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus</i> for the title, an allusion to <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>'s <i>Tractatus Theologico-Politicus.</i> Initially there were difficulties in finding a publisher for the English edition too, because Wittgenstein was insisting it appear without Russell's introduction; Cambridge University Press turned it down for that reason. Finally in 1922 an agreement was reached with Wittgenstein that Kegan Paul would print a bilingual edition with Russell's introduction and the Ramsey-Ogden translation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archiveTractatus_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-archiveTractatus-134">[134]</a></sup> This is the translation that was approved by Wittgenstein, but it is problematic in a number of ways. Wittgenstein's English was poor at the time, and Ramsey was a teenager who had only recently learned German, so philosophers often prefer to use a 1961 translation by <a href="/wiki/David_Pears" title="David Pears">David Pears</a> and Brian McGuinness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>An aim of the <i>Tractatus</i> is to reveal the relationship between language and the world: what can be said about it, and what can only be shown. Wittgenstein argues that language has an underlying logical structure, a structure that provides the limits of what can be said meaningfully. The limits of language, for Wittgenstein, are the limits of philosophy. Much of philosophy involves attempts to say the unsayable: "What we can say at all can be said clearly," he argues. Anything beyond that—religion, ethics, aesthetics, the mystical—cannot be discussed. They are not in themselves nonsensical, but any statement about them must be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grayling16_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grayling16-136">[136]</a></sup> He wrote in the preface: "The book will, therefore, draw a limit to thinking, or rather—not to thinking, but to the expression of thoughts; for, in order to draw a limit to thinking we should have to be able to think both sides of this limit (we should therefore have to be able to think what cannot be thought)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>The book is 75 pages long—"As to the shortness of the book, I am <i>awfully sorry for it</i> ... If you were to squeeze me like a lemon you would get nothing more out of me," he told Ogden—and presents seven numbered propositions (1–7), with various sub-levels (1, 1.1, 1.11):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li><i>Die Welt ist alles, was der Fall ist</i>.
<dl>
<dd>The world is everything that is the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
</li>
<li><i>Was der Fall ist, die Tatsache, ist das Bestehen von Sachverhalten</i>.
<dl>
<dd>What is the case, the fact, is the existence of atomic facts.</dd>
</dl>
</li>
<li><i>Das logische Bild der Tatsachen ist der Gedanke</i>.
<dl>
<dd>The logical picture of the facts is the thought.</dd>
</dl>
</li>
<li><i>Der Gedanke ist der sinnvolle Satz</i>.
<dl>
<dd>The thought is the signiﬁcant proposition.</dd>
</dl>
</li>
<li><i>Der Satz ist eine Wahrheitsfunktion der Elementarsätze</i>.
<dl>
<dd>Propositions are truth-functions of elementary propositions.</dd>
</dl>
</li>
<li><i>Die allgemeine Form der Wahrheitsfunktion ist: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<dl>
<dd>The general form of a truth-function is: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<li><i>Wovon man nicht sprechen kann, darüber muß man schweigen</i>.
<dl>
<dd>Whereof one cannot speak, thereof one must be silent.</dd>

</li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visit_from_Frank_Ramsey.2C_Puchberg">Visit from Frank Ramsey, Puchberg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Visit from Frank Ramsey, Puchberg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2856" data-file-width="2142" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg/170px-Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg/255px-Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg/340px-Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Frank_P._Ramsey" title="Frank P. Ramsey">Frank P. Ramsey</a> visited Wittgenstein in <a href="/wiki/Puchberg_am_Schneeberg" title="Puchberg am Schneeberg">Puchberg am Schneeberg</a> in September 1923.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In September 1922 he moved to a secondary school in a nearby village, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hassbach_(Lower_Austria)" title="Hassbach (Lower Austria)">Hassbach</a>, but considered the people there just as bad—"These people are not human <i>at all</i> but loathsome worms," he wrote to a friend—and he left after a month. In November he began work at another primary school, this time in <a href="/wiki/Puchberg_am_Schneeberg" title="Puchberg am Schneeberg">Puchberg</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Schneeberg_(Alps)" title="Schneeberg (Alps)">Schneeberg</a> mountains. There, he told Russell, the villagers were "one-quarter animal and three-quarters human."</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Frank_P._Ramsey" title="Frank P. Ramsey">Frank P. Ramsey</a> visited him on 17 September 1923 to discuss the <i>Tractatus</i>; he had agreed to write a review of it for <i>Mind</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk212_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk212-140">[140]</a></sup> He reported in a letter home that Wittgenstein was living frugally in one tiny whitewashed room that only had space for a bed, washstand, a small table, and one small hard chair. Ramsey shared an evening meal with him of coarse bread, butter, and cocoa. Wittgenstein's school hours were eight to twelve or one, and he had afternoons free.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> After Ramsey returned to Cambridge a long campaign began among Wittgenstein's friends to persuade him to return to Cambridge and away from what they saw as a hostile environment for him. He was accepting no help even from his family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk212_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk212-140">[140]</a></sup> Ramsey wrote to <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"[Wittgenstein's family] are very rich and extremely anxious to give him money or do anything for him in any way, and he rejects all their advances; even Christmas presents or presents of invalid's food, when he is ill, he sends back. And this is not because they aren't on good terms but because he won't have any money he hasn't earned ... It is an awful pity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk212_140-2"><a href="#cite_note-Monk212-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Haidbauer_incident.2C_Otterthal">Haidbauer incident, Otterthal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Haidbauer incident, Otterthal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haidbauer_incident" title="Haidbauer incident">Haidbauer incident</a></div>
<p>He moved schools again in September 1924, this time to <a href="/wiki/Otterthal" title="Otterthal">Otterthal</a>, near Trattenbach; the socialist headmaster, Josef Putre, was someone Wittgenstein had become friends with while at Trattenbach. While he was there, he wrote a 42-page pronunciation and spelling dictionary for the children, <i>Wörterbuch für Volksschulen</i>, published in Vienna in 1926 by Hölder-Pichler-Tempsky, the only book of his apart from the <i>Tractatus</i> that was published in his lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archiveTractatus_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-archiveTractatus-134">[134]</a></sup> A first edition sold in 2005 for £75,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ezard_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ezard-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>An incident occurred in April 1926 and became known as <i>Der Vorfall Haidbauer</i> (the Haidbauer incident). Josef Haidbauer was an 11-year-old pupil whose father had died and whose mother worked as a local maid. He was a slow learner, and one day Wittgenstein hit him two or three times on the head, causing him to collapse. Wittgenstein carried him to the headmaster's office, then quickly left the school, bumping into a parent, Herr Piribauer, on the way out. Piribauer had been sent for by the children when they saw Haidbauer collapse; Wittgenstein had previously pulled Piribauer's daughter, Hermine, so hard by the ears that her ears had bled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk225_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk225-143">[143]</a></sup> Piribauer said that when he met Wittgenstein in the hall that day: "I called him all the names under the sun. I told him he wasn't a teacher, he was an animal-trainer! And that I was going to fetch the police right away!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk225_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk225-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Piribauer tried to have Wittgenstein arrested, but the village's police station was empty, and when he tried again the next day he was told Wittgenstein had disappeared. On 28 April 1926, Wittgenstein handed in his resignation to Wilhelm Kundt, a local school inspector, who tried to persuade him to stay; however, Wittgenstein was adamant that his days as a schoolteacher were over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk225_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-Monk225-143">[143]</a></sup> Proceedings were initiated in May, and the judge ordered a psychiatric report; in August 1926 a letter to Wittgenstein from a friend, Ludwig Hänsel, indicates that hearings were ongoing, but nothing is known about the case after that. Alexander Waugh writes that Wittgenstein's family and their money may have had a hand in covering things up. Waugh writes that Haidbauer died shortly afterwards of haemophilia; Monk says he died when he was 14 of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leukaemia" title="Leukaemia">leukaemia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ten years later, in 1936, as part of a series of "confessions" he engaged in that year, Wittgenstein appeared without warning at the village saying he wanted to confess personally and ask for pardon from the children he had hit. He visited at least four of the children, including Hermine Piribauer, who apparently replied only with a "Ja, ja," though other former students were more hospitable. Monk writes that the purpose of these confessions was not "to hurt his pride, as a form of punishment; it was to dismantle it—to remove a barrier, as it were, that stood in the way of honest and decent thought." Of the apologies, Wittgenstein wrote, "This brought me into more settled waters... and to greater seriousness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Vienna_Circle">The Vienna Circle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: The Vienna Circle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Circle" title="Vienna Circle">Vienna Circle</a></div>
<p>The <i>Tractatus</i> was now the subject of much debate amongst philosophers, and Wittgenstein was a figure of increasing international fame. In particular, a discussion group of philosophers, scientists and mathematicians, known as the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Circle" title="Vienna Circle">Vienna Circle</a>, had built up largely as a result of the inspiration they had been given by reading the <i>Tractatus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk212_140-3"><a href="#cite_note-Monk212-140">[140]</a></sup> While it is commonly assumed that Wittgenstein was a part of the Vienna Circle, in reality, this was not actually the case. German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Hanfling" title="Oswald Hanfling">Oswald Hanfling</a> writes bluntly: "Wittgenstein was never a member of the Circle, though he was in Vienna during much of the time. Yet his influence on the Circle's thought was at least as important as that of any of its members."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-146">[146]</a></sup> From 1926, with the members of the Vienna Circle, Wittgenstein would take part in many discussions. However, during these discussions, it soon became evident that Wittgenstein held a different attitude towards philosophy than the members of the Circle whom his work had inspired. For example, during meetings of the Vienna Circle, he would express his disagreement with the group's misreading of his work by turning his back to them and reading poetry aloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In his autobiography, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Carnap" title="Rudolf Carnap">Rudolf Carnap</a> describes Wittgenstein as the thinker who gave him the greatest inspiration. However, he also wrote that "there was a striking difference between Wittgenstein's attitude toward philosophical problems and that of Schlick and myself. Our attitude toward philosophical problems was not very different from that which scientists have toward their problems." As for Wittgenstein:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>His point of view and his attitude toward people and problems, even theoretical problems, were much more similar to those of a creative artist than to those of a scientist; one might almost say, similar to those of a religious prophet or a seer... When finally, sometimes after a prolonged arduous effort, his answers came forth, his statement stood before us like a newly created piece of art or a divine revelation ... the impression he made on us was as if insight came to him as through divine inspiration, so that we could not help feeling that any sober rational comment or analysis of it would be a profanation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Haus_Wittgenstein">Haus Wittgenstein</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Haus Wittgenstein">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haus_Wittgenstein" title="Haus Wittgenstein">Haus Wittgenstein</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haus_Wittgenstein,_Stonborough_House,_Vienna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="540" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg/150px-Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg/225px-Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg/300px-Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haus_Wittgenstein,_Stonborough_House,_Vienna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wittgenstein worked on <a href="/wiki/Haus_Wittgenstein" title="Haus Wittgenstein">Haus Wittgenstein</a> between 1926 and 1929.</div>
</div>
</div>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">"I am not interested in erecting a building, but in [...] presenting to myself the foundations of all possible buildings."</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>— Wittgenstein<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-149">[149]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In 1926 Wittgenstein was again working as a gardener for a number of months, this time at the monastery of Hütteldorf, where he had also inquired about becoming a monk. His sister, Margaret, invited him to help with the design of her new townhouse in Vienna's <i>Kundmanngasse</i>. Wittgenstein, his friend <a href="/wiki/Paul_Engelmann" title="Paul Engelmann">Paul Engelmann</a>, and a team of architects developed a spare modernist house. In particular, Wittgenstein focused on the windows, doors, and radiators, demanding that every detail be exactly as he specified. When the house was nearly finished Wittgenstein had an entire ceiling raised 30mm so that the room had the exact proportions he wanted. Monk writes that "This is not so marginal as it may at first appear, for it is precisely these details that lend what is otherwise a rather plain, even ugly house its distinctive beauty."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffries_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffries-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>It took him a year to design the door handles and another to design the radiators. Each window was covered by a metal screen that weighed 150 kg, moved by a pulley Wittgenstein designed. Bernhard Leitner, author of <i>The Architecture of Ludwig Wittgenstein</i>, said there is barely anything comparable in the history of interior design: "It is as ingenious as it is expensive. A metal curtain that could be lowered into the floor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffries_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffries-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>The house was finished by December 1928 and the family gathered there at Christmas to celebrate its completion. Wittgenstein's sister Hermine wrote: "Even though I admired the house very much....It seemed indeed to be much more a dwelling for the gods."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-149">[149]</a></sup> Wittgenstein said "the house I built for Gretl is the product of a decidedly sensitive ear and <i>good</i> manners, and expression of great <i>understanding</i>... But <i>primordial</i> life, wild life striving to erupt into the open - that is lacking."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk.2C_p._240_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk.2C_p._240-151">[151]</a></sup> Monk comments that the same might be said of the technically excellent, but austere, terracotta sculpture Wittgenstein had modelled of Marguerite Respinger in 1926, and that, as Russell first noticed, this "wild life striving to be in the open" was precisely the substance of Wittgenstein's philosophical work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk.2C_p._240_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk.2C_p._240-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1929.E2.80.931941:_Fellowship_at_Cambridge">1929–1941: Fellowship at Cambridge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 1929–1941: Fellowship at Cambridge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="PhD_and_fellowship">PhD and fellowship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: PhD and fellowship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the urging of Ramsey and others, Wittgenstein returned to Cambridge in 1929. Keynes wrote in a letter to his wife: "Well, God has arrived. I met him on the 5.15 train."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Despite this fame, he could not initially work at Cambridge as he did not have a degree, so he applied as an advanced undergraduate. Russell noted that his previous residency was sufficient to fulfill eligibility requirements for a PhD, and urged him to offer the <i>Tractatus</i> as his thesis. It was examined in 1929 by Russell and Moore; at the end of the thesis defence, Wittgenstein clapped the two examiners on the shoulder and said, "Don't worry, I know you'll never understand it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Moore wrote in the examiner's report: "I myself consider that this is a work of genius; but, even if I am completely mistaken and it is nothing of the sort, it is well above the standard required for the Ph.D. degree."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Wittgenstein was appointed as a lecturer and was made a fellow of Trinity College.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anschluss">Anschluss</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Anschluss">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Anschluss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Laws" title="Nuremberg Laws">Nuremberg Laws</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mischling_Test" title="Mischling Test">Mischling Test</a></div>
<p>From 1936 to 1937, Wittgenstein lived again in Norway,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> where he worked on the <i>Philosophical Investigations</i>. In the winter of 1936/7, he delivered a series of "confessions" to close friends, most of them about minor infractions like white lies, in an effort to cleanse himself. In 1938, he travelled to Ireland to visit <a href="/wiki/Maurice_O%27Connor_Drury" title="Maurice O'Connor Drury">Maurice O'Connor Drury</a>, a friend who became a psychiatrist, and considered such training himself, with the intention of abandoning philosophy for it. The visit to Ireland was at the same time a response to the invitation of the then Irish <a href="/wiki/Taoiseach" title="Taoiseach">Taoiseach</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89amon_de_Valera" title="Éamon de Valera">Éamon de Valera</a>, himself a former mathematics teacher. De Valera hoped Wittgenstein's presence would contribute to the <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Institute_for_Advanced_Studies" title="Dublin Institute for Advanced Studies">Dublin Institute for Advanced Studies</a> which he was soon to set up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>While he was in Ireland in March 1938, Germany annexed Austria in the <i>Anschluss</i>; the Viennese Wittgenstein was now a citizen of the enlarged Germany and a Jew under the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Laws" title="Nuremberg Laws">1935 Nuremberg racial laws</a>, because three of his grandparents had been born as Jews. The Nuremberg Laws classified people as Jews (<i>Volljuden</i>) if they had three or four Jewish grandparents, and as mixed blood (<i><a href="/wiki/Mischling" title="Mischling">Mischling</a></i>) if they had one or two. It meant <i>inter alia</i> that the Wittgensteins were restricted in whom they could marry or have sex with, and where they could work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waugh137_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waugh137-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Anschluss, his brother Paul left almost immediately for England, and later the US. The Nazis discovered his relationship with Hilde Schania, a brewer's daughter with whom he had had two children but whom he had never married, though he did later. Because she was not Jewish, he was served with a summons for <i><a href="/wiki/Rassenschande" title="Rassenschande">Rassenschande</a></i> (racial defilement). He told no one he was leaving the country, except for Hilde who agreed to follow him. He left so suddenly and quietly that for a time people believed he was the fourth Wittgenstein brother to have committed suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wittgenstein began to investigate acquiring British or Irish citizenship with the help of Keynes, and apparently had to confess to his friends in England that he had earlier misrepresented himself to them as having just one Jewish grandparent, when in fact he had three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>A few days before the invasion of Poland, Hitler personally granted <i>Mischling</i> status to the Wittgenstein siblings. In 1939 there were 2,100 applications for this, and Hitler granted only 12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Anthony Gottlieb writes that the pretext was that their paternal grandfather had been the bastard son of a German prince, which allowed the Reichsbank to claim the gold, foreign currency, and stocks held in Switzerland by a Wittgenstein trust. Gretl, an American citizen by marriage, started the negotiations over the racial status of their grandfather, and the family's large foreign currency reserves were used as a bargaining tool. Paul had escaped to Switzerland and then the US in July 1938, and disagreed with the negotiations, leading to a permanent split between the siblings. After the war, when Paul was performing in Vienna, he did not visit Hermine who was dying there, and he had no further contact with Ludwig or Gretl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gottlieb_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gottlieb-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Professor_of_philosophy">Professor of philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Professor of philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After G. E. Moore resigned the chair in philosophy in 1939, Wittgenstein was elected, and acquired British citizenship soon afterwards. In July 1939 he travelled to Vienna to assist Gretl and his other sisters, visiting Berlin for one day to meet an official of the Reichsbank. After this, he travelled to New York to persuade Paul, whose agreement was required, to back the scheme. The required Befreiung was granted in August 1939. The unknown amount signed over to the Nazis by the Wittgenstein family, a week or so before the outbreak of war, included amongst many other assets, 1700 kg of gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> There is a report Wittgenstein visited Moscow a second time in 1939, travelling from Berlin, and again met the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Sofya_Yanovskaya" title="Sofya Yanovskaya">Sophia Janowskaya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>Norman Malcolm, at the time a post-graduate research fellow at Cambridge, describes his first impressions of Wittgenstein in 1938:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"At a meeting of the Moral Science Club, after the paper for the evening was read and the discussion started, someone began to stammer a remark. He had extreme difficulty in expressing himself and his words were unintelligible to me. I whispered to my neighbour, 'Who's that?': he replied, 'Wittgenstein'. I was astonished because I had expected the famous author of the <i>Tractatus</i> to be an elderly man, whereas this man looked <i>young</i> – perhaps about 35. (His actual age was 49.) His face was lean and brown, his profile was aquiline and strikingly beautiful, his head was covered with a curly mass of brown hair. I observed the respectful attention that everyone in the room paid to him. After this unsuccessful beginning he did not speak for a time but was obviously struggling with his thoughts. His look was concentrated, he made striking gestures with his hands as if he was discoursing... Whether lecturing or conversing privately, Wittgenstein always spoke emphatically and with a distinctive intonation. He spoke excellent English, with the accent of an educated Englishman, although occasional Germanisms would appear in his constructions. His voice was resonant... His words came out, not fluently, but with great force. Anyone who heard him say anything knew that this was a singular person. His face was remarkably mobile and expressive when he talked. His eyes were deep and often fierce in their expression. His whole personality was commanding, even imperial."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Describing Wittgenstein's lecture program, Malcolm continues:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"It is hardly correct to speak of these meetings as 'lectures', although this is what Wittgenstein called them. For one thing, he was carrying on original research in these meetings... Often the meetings consisted mainly of dialogue. Sometimes, however, when he was trying to draw a thought out of himself, he would prohibit, with a peremptory motion of the hand, any questions or remarks. There were frequent and prolonged periods of silence, with only an occasional mutter from Wittgenstein, and the stillest attention from the others. During these silences, Wittgenstein was extremely tense and active. His gaze was concentrated; his face was alive; his hands made arresting movements; his expression was stern. One knew that one was in the presence of extreme seriousness, absorption, and force of intellect... Wittgenstein was a frightening person at these classes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>After work, the philosopher would often relax by watching <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Movie" title="Western Movie">Westerns</a>, where he preferred to sit at the very front of the cinema, or reading detective stories especially the ones written by <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Davis" title="Norbert Davis">Norbert Davis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Norman Malcolm wrote that Wittgenstein would rush to the cinema when class ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>By this time, Wittgenstein's view on the <a href="/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundations of mathematics</a> had changed considerably. In his early 20s, Wittgenstein had thought logic could provide a solid foundation, and he had even considered updating Russell and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Whitehead</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Principia_Mathematica" title="Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a></i>. Now he denied there were any mathematical facts to be discovered. He gave a series of lectures on mathematics, discussing this and other topics, documented in a book, with lectures by Wittgenstein and discussions between him and several students, including the young <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a> who described Wittgenstein as "a <i>very</i> peculiar man". The two had many discussions about the relationship between computational logic and everyday notions of truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_and_Guy.27s_Hospital">World War II and Guy's Hospital</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: World War II and Guy's Hospital">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Monk writes that Wittgenstein found it intolerable that a war was going on and he was teaching philosophy. He grew angry when any of his students wanted to become professional philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 1941 he asked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Ryle_(professor)" title="John Ryle (professor)">John Ryle</a>, the brother of the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Ryle" title="Gilbert Ryle">Gilbert Ryle</a>, if he could get a manual job at <a href="/wiki/Guy%27s_Hospital" title="Guy's Hospital">Guy's Hospital</a> in London. John Ryle was professor of medicine at Cambridge and had been involved in helping Guy's prepare for <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">the Blitz</a>. Wittgenstein told Ryle he would die slowly if left at Cambridge, and he would rather die quickly. He started working at Guy's shortly afterwards as a dispensary porter, meaning that he delivered drugs from the pharmacy to the wards—where he apparently advised the patients not to take them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk431_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk431-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hospital staff were not told he was one of the world's most famous philosophers, though some of the medical staff did recognize him—at least one had attended Moral Sciences Club meetings—but they were discreet. "Good God, don't tell anybody who I am!" Wittgenstein begged one of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Some of them nevertheless called him Professor Wittgenstein, and he was allowed to dine with the doctors. He wrote on 1 April 1942: "I no longer feel any hope for the future of my life. It is as though I had before me nothing more than a long stretch of living death. I cannot imagine any future for me other than a ghastly one. Friendless and joyless."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk431_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk431-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>He had developed a friendship with Keith Kirk, a working-class teenage friend of <a href="/wiki/Francis_Skinner" title="Francis Skinner">Francis Skinner</a>, the mathematics undergraduate he had had a relationship with until Skinner's death in 1941 from polio. Skinner had given up academia, thanks at least in part to Wittgenstein's influence, and had been working as a mechanic in 1939, with Kirk as his apprentice. Kirk and Wittgenstein struck up a friendship, with Wittgenstein giving him lessons in physics to help him pass a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_and_Guilds" title="City and Guilds">City and Guilds</a> exam. During his period of loneliness at Guy's he wrote in his diary: "For ten days I've heard nothing more from K, even though I pressed him a week ago for news. I think that he has perhaps broken with me. A <i>tragic</i> thought!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk443_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Monk443-38">[38]</a></sup> Kirk had in fact got married, and they never saw one another again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk443_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Monk443-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Wittgenstein was at Guy's he met Basil Reeve, a young doctor with an interest in philosophy, who, with R. T. Grant, was studying the effect of shock on air-raid casualties. When the blitz ended there were fewer casualties to study. In November 1942, Grant and Reeve moved to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Victoria_Infirmary" title="Royal Victoria Infirmary">Royal Victoria Infirmary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a>, to study road traffic and industrial casualties. Grant offered Wittgenstein a position as a laboratory assistant at a wage of £4 per week, and he lived in Newcastle (at 28 Brandling Park, <a href="/wiki/Jesmond" title="Jesmond">Jesmond</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schardt_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schardt-173">[173]</a></sup>) from 29 April 1943 until February 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk447_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk447-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1947.E2.80.931951:_Final_years">1947–1951: Final years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: 1947–1951: Final years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="rquote floatleft" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:left; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;"><i>Death is not an event in life: we do not live to experience death. If we take eternity to mean not infinite temporal duration but timelessness, then eternal life belongs to those who live in the present. Our life has no end in the way in which our visual field has no limits.</i></td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>— Wittgenstein, Tractatus, 6.431</cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Wittgenstein resigned the professorship at Cambridge in 1947 to concentrate on his writing, and in 1947 and 1948 travelled to <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, staying at Ross's Hotel in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> and at a farmhouse in <a href="/wiki/Redcross" title="Redcross">Redcross</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Wicklow" title="County Wicklow">County Wicklow</a>, where he began the manuscript volume MS 137, Band R. Seeking solitude he moved to <i>Rosro</i>, a holiday cottage in <a href="/wiki/Connemara" title="Connemara">Connemara</a> owned by Maurice O'Connor-Drury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archivefinalyears_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-archivefinalyears-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title=" Dead link since May 2015">dead link</span></a></i>]</span></sup></p>
<p>He also accepted an invitation from <a href="/wiki/Norman_Malcolm" title="Norman Malcolm">Norman Malcolm</a>, then professor at Cornell University, to stay with him and his wife for several months at <a href="/wiki/Ithaca,_New_York" title="Ithaca, New York">Ithaca, New York</a>. He made the trip in April 1949, although he told Malcolm he was too unwell to do philosophical work: "I haven't done any work since the beginning of March &amp; I haven't had the strength of even trying to do any." A doctor in Dublin had diagnosed anaemia and prescribed iron and liver pills. The details of Wittgenstein's stay in America are recounted in Norman Malcolm's <i>Ludwig Wittgenstein: A Memoir</i>. During his summer in America, Wittgenstein began his epistemological discussions, in particular his engagement with <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">philosophical skepticism</a>, that would eventually become the final fragments <i><a href="/wiki/On_Certainty" title="On Certainty">On Certainty</a></i>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque,_76_Storey%27s_Way,_Cambridge,_UK.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg/220px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg/330px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg/440px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque,_76_Storey%27s_Way,_Cambridge,_UK.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The plaque at "Storey's End", 76 <a href="/wiki/Storey%27s_Way" title="Storey's Way">Storey's Way</a>, Cambridge, where Wittgenstein died.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>He returned to London, where he was diagnosed with an inoperable <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a>, which had spread to his bone marrow. He spent the next two months in Vienna, where his sister Hermine died on 11 February 1950; he went to see her every day, but she was hardly able to speak or recognize him. "Great loss for me and all of us," he wrote. "Greater than I would have thought." He moved around a lot after Hermine's death staying with various friends: to Cambridge in April 1950, where he stayed with <a href="/wiki/Georg_Henrik_von_Wright" title="Georg Henrik von Wright">G. H. von Wright</a>; to London to stay with <a href="/wiki/Rush_Rhees" title="Rush Rhees">Rush Rhees</a>; then to Oxford to see <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Anscombe" title="Elizabeth Anscombe">Elizabeth Anscombe</a>, writing to Norman Malcolm that he was hardly doing any philosophy. He went to Norway in August with Ben Richards, then returned to Cambridge, where on 27 November he moved into <i>Storey's End</i> at 76 <a href="/wiki/Storey%27s_Way" title="Storey's Way">Storey's Way</a>, the home of his doctor, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Vaughan_Bevan" title="Edward Vaughan Bevan">Edward Bevan</a>, and his wife Joan; he had told them he did not want to die in a hospital, so they said he could spend his last days in their home instead. Joan at first was afraid of Wittgenstein, but they soon became good friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archivefinalyears_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-archivefinalyears-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the beginning of 1951, it was clear that he had little time left. He wrote a new will in Oxford on 29 January, naming Rhees as his executor, and Anscombe and von Wright his literary administrators, and wrote to Norman Malcolm that month to say, "My mind's completely dead. This isn't a complaint, for I don't really suffer from it. I know that life must have an end once and that mental life can cease before the rest does."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> In February he returned to the Bevans' home to work on MS 175 and MS 176. These and other manuscripts were later published as <i><a href="/wiki/Remarks_on_Colour" title="Remarks on Colour">Remarks on Colour</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/On_Certainty" title="On Certainty">On Certainty</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archivefinalyears_175-2"><a href="#cite_note-archivefinalyears-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title=" Dead link since May 2015">dead link</span></a></i>]</span></sup> He wrote to Malcolm on 16 April 13 days before his death: "An extraordinary thing happened to me. About a month ago I suddenly found myself in the right frame of mind for doing philosophy. I had been <i>absolutely</i> certain that I'd never again be able to do it. It's the first time after more than 2 years that the curtain in my brain has gone up.—Of course, so far I've only worked for about 5 weeks &amp; it may be all over by tomorrow; but it bucks me up a lot now."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malcolm80_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malcolm80-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="1296" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg/220px-Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg/330px-Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg/440px-Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wittgenstein's grave at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascension_Parish_Burial_Ground,_Cambridge" title="Ascension Parish Burial Ground, Cambridge">Ascension Parish Burial Ground</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Wittgenstein began work on his final manuscript, MS 177, on 25 April 1951. It was his 62nd birthday on 26 April. He went for a walk the next afternoon, and wrote his last entry that day, 27 April. That evening, he became very ill; when his doctor told him he might live only a few days, he reportedly replied, "Good!" Joan stayed with him throughout that night, and just before losing consciousness for the last time on 28 April, he told her: "Tell them I've had a wonderful life." Norman Malcolm describes this as a "...strangely moving utterance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malcolm80_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Malcolm80-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>Four of Wittgenstein's former students arrived at his bedside—Ben Richards, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Anscombe" title="Elizabeth Anscombe">Elizabeth Anscombe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yorick_Smythies" title="Yorick Smythies">Yorick Smythies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maurice_O%27Connor_Drury" title="Maurice O'Connor Drury">Maurice O'Connor Drury</a>. Anscombe and Smythies were Catholics; and, at the latter's request, a Dominican friar, Father <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Pepler" title="Conrad Pepler">Conrad Pepler</a>, also attended. They were at first unsure what Wittgenstein would have wanted, but then remembered he had said he hoped his Catholic friends would pray for him, so they did, and he was pronounced dead shortly afterwards.</p>
<p>Wittgenstein was given a Catholic burial at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascension_Parish_Burial_Ground,_Cambridge" title="Ascension Parish Burial Ground, Cambridge">Parish of the Ascension Burial Ground</a> in Cambridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Drury later said he had been troubled ever since about whether that was the right thing to do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monk576_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monk576-179">[179]</a></sup> In 2015 the ledger gravestone was refurbished by the British Wittgenstein Society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>On his religious views, Wittgenstein was said to be greatly interested in <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a> and was sympathetic to it. In his university years, he expressed belief in the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">Resurrection of Jesus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-res_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-res-181">[181]</a></sup> However, he did not consider himself to be a Catholic. According to <a href="/wiki/Norman_Malcolm" title="Norman Malcolm">Norman Malcolm</a>, Wittgenstein saw Catholicism as more of a way of life than as a set of beliefs he personally held, considering that he did not accept any religious faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wittgenstein was said to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agnostic" title="Agnostic">agnostic</a>, in a qualified sense, in the last years of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1953:_Publication_of_the_Philosophical_Investigations">1953: Publication of the <i>Philosophical Investigations</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: 1953: Publication of the Philosophical Investigations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Investigations" title="Philosophical Investigations">Philosophical Investigations</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Language-game" title="Language-game">Language-game</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Private_language_argument" title="Private language argument">Private language argument</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaninchen_und_Ente.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1414" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Kaninchen_und_Ente.png/220px-Kaninchen_und_Ente.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Kaninchen_und_Ente.png/330px-Kaninchen_und_Ente.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Kaninchen_und_Ente.png/440px-Kaninchen_und_Ente.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kaninchen_und_Ente.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration of a "<a href="/wiki/Rabbit%E2%80%93duck_illusion" title="Rabbit–duck illusion">duckrabbit</a>", discussed in the <i>Philosophical Investigations</i>, section XI, part II</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Blue_and_Brown_Books" title="Blue and Brown Books">Blue Book</a></i>, a set of notes dictated to his class at Cambridge in 1933–1934, contains the seeds of Wittgenstein's later thoughts on language, and is widely read as a turning-point in his philosophy of language.</p>
<p><i>Philosophical Investigations</i> was published in two parts in 1953. Most of Part I was ready for printing in 1946, but Wittgenstein withdrew the manuscript from his publisher. The shorter Part II was added by his editors, Elizabeth Anscombe and Rush Rhees. Wittgenstein asks the reader to think of language as a multiplicity of language-games within which parts of language develop and function. He argues that philosophical problems are bewitchments that arise from philosophers' misguided attempts to consider the meaning of words independently of their context, usage, and grammar, what he called "language gone on holiday."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Wittgenstein, philosophical problems arise when language is forced from its proper home into a metaphysical environment, where all the familiar and necessary landmarks and contextual clues are removed. He describes this metaphysical environment as like being on frictionless ice: where the conditions are apparently perfect for a philosophically and logically perfect language, all philosophical problems can be solved without the muddying effects of everyday contexts; but where, precisely because of the lack of friction, language can in fact do no work at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> Wittgenstein argues that philosophers must leave the frictionless ice and return to the "rough ground" of ordinary language in use. Much of the <i>Investigations</i> consists of examples of how the first false steps can be avoided, so that philosophical problems are dissolved, rather than solved: "the clarity we are aiming at is indeed <i>complete</i> clarity. But this simply means that the philosophical problems should <i>completely</i> disappear."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Wittgenstein left a voluminous archive of unpublished papers, including 83 manuscripts, 46 typescripts and 11 dictations, amounting to an estimated 20,000 pages. Choosing among repeated drafts, revisions, corrections and loose notes editorial work has found nearly one third of the total suitable for print.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> An Internet facility hosted by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bergen" title="University of Bergen">University of Bergen</a> allows access to images of almost all the material and to search the available transcriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> In 2011 two new boxes of Wittgenstein papers were found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Investigations" title="Philosophical Investigations">Philosophical Investigations</a></i> was the only nearly finished project and the book was published in 1953.</p>
<p>In 1999 a survey among American university and college teachers ranked the <i>Investigations</i> as the most important book of 20th-century philosophy, standing out as "the one crossover masterpiece in twentieth-century philosophy, appealing across diverse specializations and philosophical orientations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> The <i>Investigations</i> also ranked 54th on a list of most influential twentieth-century works in cognitive science prepared by the University of Minnesota's Center for Cognitive Sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milproj_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-milproj-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peter_Hacker" title="Peter Hacker">Peter Hacker</a> argues that Wittgenstein's influence on 20th-century analytical philosophy can be attributed to his early influence on the Vienna Circle and later influence on the Oxford "ordinary language" school and Cambridge philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hacker_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-hacker-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wittgenstein's influence has been felt in nearly every field of the humanities and social sciences, yet there are diverging interpretations of his thought. In the words of his friend and colleague <a href="/wiki/Georg_Henrik_von_Wright" title="Georg Henrik von Wright">Georg Henrik von Wright</a>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>He was of the opinion... that his ideas were generally misunderstood and distorted even by those who professed to be his disciples. He doubted he would be better understood in the future. He once said he felt as though he was writing for people who would think in a different way, breathe a different air of life, from that of present-day men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpreters">Interpreters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Interpreters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since Wittgenstein's death, scholarly interpretations of his philosophy have differed. Scholars have differed on the continuity between "early" and "late" Wittgenstein (that is, the difference between his views expressed in the <i>Tractatus</i> and those in <i>Philosophical Investigations</i>), with some seeing the two as starkly disparate and others stressing the gradual transition between the two works through analysis of Wittgenstein's unpublished papers (the <i><a href="/wiki/Nachlass" title="Nachlass">Nachlass</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glock_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glock-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>One significant debate in Wittgenstein scholarship concerns the work of interpreters who are referred to under the banner of the <a href="/wiki/New_Wittgenstein" title="New Wittgenstein">New Wittgenstein</a> school such as <a href="/wiki/Cora_Diamond" title="Cora Diamond">Cora Diamond</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alice_Crary" title="Alice Crary">Alice Crary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_F._Conant" title="James F. Conant">James F. Conant</a>. While the <i>Tractatus</i>, particularly in its conclusion, seems paradoxical and self-undermining, New Wittgenstein scholars advance a "therapeutic" understanding of Wittgenstein's work—"an understanding of Wittgenstein as aspiring, not to advance metaphysical theories, but rather to help us work ourselves out of confusions we become entangled in when philosophizing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> To support this goal, the New Wittgenstein scholars propose a reading of the <i>Tractatus</i> as "plain nonsense"—arguing it does not attempt to convey a substantive philosophical project but instead simply tries to push the reader to abandon philosophical speculation. The therapeutic approach traces its roots to the philosophical work of <a href="/wiki/John_Wisdom" title="John Wisdom">John Wisdom</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> and the review of <i>The Blue Book</i> written by <a href="/wiki/Oets_Kolk_Bouwsma" title="Oets Kolk Bouwsma">Oets Kolk Bouwsma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glock_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glock-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>The therapeutic approach is not without critics: <a href="/wiki/Hans-Johann_Glock" title="Hans-Johann Glock">Hans-Johann Glock</a> argues that the "plain nonsense" reading of the <i>Tractatus</i> "...is at odds with the external evidence, writings and conversations in which Wittgenstein states that the <i>Tractatus</i> is committed to the idea of ineffable insight."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glock_195-2"><a href="#cite_note-Glock-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="refbegin columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">
<p>A collection of Ludwig Wittgenstein's manuscripts is held by Trinity College, Cambridge.</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Logisch-Philosophische Abhandlung</i>, Annalen der Naturphilosophie, 14 (1921)
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Tractatus_Logico-Philosophicus" title="Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus">Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus</a></i> [<i>TLP</i>], translated by C.K. Ogden (1922)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i>Philosophische Untersuchungen</i> (1953)
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Investigations" title="Philosophical Investigations">Philosophical Investigations</a></i> [<i>PI</i>], translated by G.E.M. Anscombe (1953)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i>Bemerkungen über die Grundlagen der Mathematik</i>, ed. by G.H. von Wright, R. Rhees, and G.E.M. Anscombe (1956), a selection of his work on the philosophy of logic and mathematics between 1937 and 1944.
<ul>
<li><i>Remarks on the Foundations of Mathematics</i>, translated by G.E.M. Anscombe, rev. ed. (1978)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i>Bemerkungen über die Philosophie der Psychologie</i>, ed. G.E.M. Anscombe and G.H. von Wright (1980)
<ul>
<li><i>Remarks on the Philosophy of Psychology, Vols. 1 and 2</i>, translated by G.E.M. Anscombe, ed. G.E.M. Anscombe and G.H. von Wright (1980), a selection of which makes up <i>Zettel</i>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i>The Blue and Brown Books</i> (1958), notes dictated in English to Cambridge students in 1933–1935.</li>
<li><i>Philosophische Bemerkungen</i>, ed. by Rush Rhees (1964)
<ul>
<li><i>Philosophical Remarks</i> (1975)</li>
<li><i>Philosophical Grammar</i> (1978)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i>Bemerkungen über die Farben</i>, ed. by G.E.M. Anscombe (1977)
<ul>
<li><i>Remarks on Colour</i> (1991), remarks on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goethe" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Theory_of_Colours" title="Theory of Colours">Theory of Colours</a></i>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_Certainty" title="On Certainty">On Certainty</a></i>, collection of aphorisms discussing the relation between knowledge and certainty, extremely influential in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_action" title="Philosophy of action">philosophy of action</a>.</li>
<li><i>Culture and Value</i>, collection of personal remarks about various cultural issues, such as religion and music, as well as critique of <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="Søren Kierkegaard">Søren Kierkegaard</a>'s philosophy.</li>
<li><i>Zettel</i>, collection of Wittgenstein's thoughts in fragmentary/"diary entry" format as with <i>On Certainty</i> and <i>Culture and Value</i>.</li>
</ul>

Coffey's <i>Science of Logic</i></a> (1913): a polemical book review, written in 1912 for the March 1913 issue of <i>The Cambridge Review</i> when Wittgenstein was an undergraduate studying with Russell. The review is the earliest public record of Wittgenstein's philosophical views.</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 33em; -webkit-column-width: 33em; column-width: 33em; list-style-type: decimal;">

<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text">Brigitte Hamann argues in <i>Hitler's Vienna</i> (1996) that Hitler was bound to have laid eyes on Wittgenstein, because the latter was so conspicuous, though she told <i>Focus</i> magazine they were in different classes, and she agrees with Monk that they would have had nothing to do with one another. See Hamann, Brigitte and Thornton, Thomas. <i>Hitler's Vienna: A Dictator's Apprenticeship</i>. Oxford University Press, 2000, pp. 15–16, 79, and Thiede, Roger. <a class="external text" href="http://www.focus.de/auto/neuheiten/zeitgeschichte-phantom-wittgenstein_aid_169829.html" rel="nofollow">"Phantom Wittgenstein"</a>, <i>Focus</i> magazine, 16 March 1998.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thiede, Roger. <a class="external text" href="http://www.focus.de/auto/neuheiten/zeitgeschichte-phantom-wittgenstein_aid_169829.html" rel="nofollow">"Phantom Wittgenstein"</a>, <i>Focus</i> magazine, 16 March 1998.</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text">The German Federal Archives says the image was taken "circa 1901"; it identifies the class as 1B and the teacher as Oskar Langer. See the full image and description at the <a class="external text" href="http://www.bild.bundesarchiv.de/cross-search/search/_1283821026/" rel="nofollow">Bundesarchiv</a>. Retrieved 6 September 2010. The archive gives the date as circa 1901, but wrongly calls it the Realschule in Leonding, near Linz. Hitler attended primary school in Leonding, but from September 1901 went to the Realschule in Linz itself. See Kershaw, Ian. <i>Hitler, 1889-1936</i>. W. W. Norton &amp; Company, 2000, p. 16ff.</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">Christoph Haidacher and Richard Schober write that Langer taught at the school from 1884 until 1901; see Haidacher, Christoph and Schober, Richard. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?ei=4tyFTJ-CLtGknQfJqOHhAQ&amp;ct=result&amp;id=XqQUAQAAIAAJ&amp;dq=%22Oskar+langer%22+hitler&amp;q=%22Oskar+langer%22#search_anchor" rel="nofollow"><i>Von Stadtstaaten und Imperien</i></a>, Universitätsverlag Wagner, 2006, p. 140.</span></li>

<li><span class="reference-text">Also see Robert Hanna, <a class="external text" href="http://www.colorado.edu/philosophy/paper_hanna_kant_wittgenstein_and_transcendental_philosophy_may11.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Kant, Wittgenstein, and Transcendental Philosophy"</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20130523143901/http://www.colorado.edu/philosophy/paper_hanna_kant_wittgenstein_and_transcendental_philosophy_may11.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 23 May 2013 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>..</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-119">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Iain_King" title="Iain King">King</a> writes: "... the two halves of his war service seem to be reflected in a change of writing style. Protected from danger until spring 1916, his words were dry, abstract, and logical. Only when he was in the midst of action did he confront ethics and aesthetics, concluding their ‘truths’ could only be shown, not stated." - taken from <a class="external text" href="http://www.military-history.org/articles/thinkers-at-war-wittgenstein.htm" rel="nofollow">'Thinker at War' article on Wittgenstein (link)</a>, accessed 23 July 2014.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-archiveTractatus-134"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-archiveTractatus_134-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-archiveTractatus_134-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.wittgen-cam.ac.uk/biogre6.html" rel="nofollow">"Ludwig Wittgenstein: Tractatus and Teaching"</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100213064904/http://www.wittgen-cam.ac.uk/biogre6.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 13 February 2010 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>., Cambridge Wittgenstein archive. <li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, Ramsey translated "<i>Sachverhalt</i>" and "<i>Sachlage</i>" as "atomic fact" and "state of affairs" respectively. But Wittgenstein discusses non-existent "<i>Sachverhalten</i>", and there cannot be a non-existent fact. Pears and McGuinness made a number of changes, including translating "<i>Sachverhalt</i>" as "state of affairs" and "<i>Sachlage</i>" as <i>situation</i>. The new translation is often preferred, but some philosophers use the original, in part because Wittgenstein approved it, and because it avoids the idiomatic English of Pears-McGuinness. See:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text">White, Roger. <i>Wittgenstein's Tractatus logico-philosophicus</i>. Continuum International Publishing Group, 2006, p. 145.</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">For a discussion about the relative merits of the translations, see Morris, Michael Rowland. "Introduction", <i>Routledge philosophy guidebook to Wittgenstein and the Tractatus</i>. Taylor &amp; Francis, 2008; and Nelson, John O. <a class="external text" href="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/1467-9205.00092/abstract" rel="nofollow">"Is the Pears-McGuinness translation of the Tractatus really superior to Ogden's and Ramsey's?"</a>, <i>Philosophical Investigations</i>, 22:2, April 1999.</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">See the three versions (Wittgenstein's German, published 1921; Ramsey-Ogden's translation, published 1922; and the Pears-McGuinness translation, published 1961) side by side <a class="external text" href="http://people.umass.edu/phil335-klement-2/tlp/tlp.html#bodytext" rel="nofollow">here</a> (<i>TLP</i>, University of Massachusetts). Retrieved 4 September 2010.</span></li>
</ul>
